    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_150773116" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/150773116" />
    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/150773116?book_show_action=true">Feb 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye">Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312427481" href="/book/show/1940878.The_Savage_Detectives">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2012">read-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews-5-stars">reviews-5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=bolano">bolano</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer150773116" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7796742403861555862"><b>What’s a Giggle Amongst Family and Friends?</b><br/><br/>I bought this book 15 months ago. I finished it yesterday. It started off as a crisp, thin-leafed semi-brick whose 648 pages intimidated me. I only got the courage to read it when a discussion group gave me the impetus I needed. Now, it sits less crisp, but read, on my desk, wondering who will read it next. Like me, it’s 15 months older, but we are both easing into middle age and are still making new friends. We two are friends now, as if we’ve known ea</span>
  <span id="freeText7796742403861555862" style="display:none"><b>What’s a Giggle Amongst Family and Friends?</b><br><br>I bought this book 15 months ago. I finished it yesterday. It started off as a crisp, thin-leafed semi-brick whose 648 pages intimidated me. I only got the courage to read it when a discussion group gave me the impetus I needed. Now, it sits less crisp, but read, on my desk, wondering who will read it next. Like me, it’s 15 months older, but we are both easing into middle age and are still making new friends. We two are friends now, as if we’ve known each other since adolescence. When I pick it up and flick through the pages, I notice my pencilled notes, and a page that I accidentally folded over when I closed it clumsily before putting it down and going to sleep one night. There’s a mysterious stain at the edge of the last 20 pages. Initially, I’d hoped it was water and I tried unsuccessfully to dry it, but I think it came too close to the furniture oil on a paper towel that I had used, ironically, to remove a water bottle stain on my desk during the week. We can’t expect to age without blemish. During the week, one of our daughters co-starred in a musical play. The other wrote an elegant letter of resignation from her part-time job, so that she could spend the next four months concentrating on her final year exams at secondary school. (Her employer thanked her for her letter and said she could have her job back any time she wanted it.) Both girls’ school netball teams were beaten by a stronger school this weekend. FM Sushi’s team lost narrowly by three. ("Though you think you did the job wrong/ You did it great.”) I scored the games and tried not to talk so much that my scoring suffered. I wondered what I would write in my review. I thought up things to say in the Cabbage Detectives Interviews. I giggled a lot. I’m going to miss that giggling.<br><br>Here is the Discussion Group:<br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/72746-roberto-bolano-s-the-savage-detectives">http://www.goodreads.com/group/show/7...</a><br><br>Make sure you visit our "homage", "The Cabbage Detectives (Interviews)":<br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/964407-the-interviews">http://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/9...</a><br><br><br><br><b>Oral History</b><br><br>Eighty percent of “TSD” by volume is written in an interview format that reminded me of the first time I ever encountered an oral history (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14606.Edie_American_Girl" title="Edie American Girl by Jean Stein" rel="nofollow noopener">Edie: American Girl</a>, an early biography of Edie Sedgwick).<br><br>I was captivated by this style and the book, and still am.<br><br>In effect, it was a collective biography of Andy Warhol’s Factory scene, told by insiders, though obviously the focus was Edie.<br><br>It was probably assembled from hundreds of interviews, lasting thousands of hours and resulting in millions of words.<br><br>Then it was distilled into one book of lasting crystalline beauty.<br><br><b>Studied Movements</b><br><br>I feel the same way about “TSD”.<br><br>You would normally expect a biography to be a study of one life lived. This is a study of multiple lives lived to the fullest.<br><br>It’s ostensibly about Arturo Belano and Ulises Lima, two alter egos of the author, Roberto Bolano.<br><br>However, in contrast to “Edie”, it gives the impression of being more about a fictitious scene (a radical poetry movement called “The Visceral Realists”) than these two individuals who were pivotal in it.<br><br>Still, we learn a lot about these two, while listening to the tales and concerns of the interviewees.<br><br>It’s interesting that Belano and Lima are not interviewees, although there is no overt suggestion that they, like Edie, were dead at the time.<br><br><b>Those Damned Accretions</b><br><br>It’s customary for the author of an oral history to superimpose a narrative or some kind of chronological or thematic timeline over the top of the interviews.<br><br>If there is a thematic structure to the narrative of “TSD”, I am not conscious of it after just one reading.<br><br>I don’t really need one to enjoy it. For me, the book is like meeting someone and learning about them spontaneously “over time”, but not necessarily chronologically.<br><br>Over time, your knowledge of your friend, together with the detail of your friendship, grows by accretion, as if a whole is growing piece by piece in the hands of some cosmic potter (man!).<br><br>So it didn’t really matter to me that the Interviews didn’t seem to be going anywhere obvious, nor did it matter how long the Interviews ended up being in total. <br><br>I was just delighted to meet this many interesting people, as if it was one long party starting in the afternoon, going full pelt through the night and winding up in the early hours of the morning as the sun of a new day emerged above the horizon.<br><br><b>The Anthology of Life</b><br><br>Each interview is a vignette, a portrait. Collectively, they make up pictures at an exhibition. <br><br>We walk through the gallery, this pantheon housing the gods of Visceral Realism, observing each work, building an impression of the exhibition as a whole. <br><br>Like art works or an anthology of verse, it doesn’t matter what order we experience them in. The important thing is the lasting impression. Does it really matter that we encountered A before B, or B before A?<br><br><b>Measure for Measure</b><br><br>The most important impression is the vitality of the lives we are witnessing, not the sequence; the chaos, not the order.<br><br>The experience of reading the book is organic, not mechanical.<br><br>Bolano seems to be at odds with measurement. He explores some of these issues in a mock sexual context.<br><br>As the pimp Alberto shows us, it’s not the length of a penis that matters, it’s how you use it. <br><br>Similarly, it’s arguable that the intensity of an orgasm is more important than its duration, measured, like Dolores, in terms of “one Mississippi, two Mississippi”.<br><br>Lastly, the experience of being a poet and writing poetry is about much more than mastering the meter of the verse.<br><br>Perhaps, there is no better measure of pleasure than the pleasure itself.<br><br><b>These Things Happen</b><br><br>Similarly, time cannot be measured except in terms of the passage of something else (e.g., distance).<br><br>In a way, we’re not here for a long time, just a good time.<br><br>Yet, vitality reverberates in motion or movement.<br><br>Like Marcel Duchamp’s painting “Nude Descending a Staircase” (which is mentioned in the novel), Bolano depicts a sense of movement in the one frame, the novel as a whole.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1379751107i/132256._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="500" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Both the past and the future are superimposed on and appear in the present.<br><br>The passage of time can be observed in the present. <br><br>I keep the past alive by remembering it in the present.<br><br>I keep the future alive by anticipating it.<br><br>If I am alive, I move.<br><br>When the movement ceases, so does life.<br><br><b>You are the Stars that Guide Me and Light My Way</b><br><br>Despite my feelings about the absence of narrative structure, I still think there is a meta-structure at work in the novel.<br><br>Bolano as author is implicitly present in the narrative as Belano and Lima. <br><br>However, the tale is ostensibly told by the interviewees.<br><br>The self is defined in terms of the others; just as importantly, the self is defined <i>by</i> the others.<br><br>In contrast to Descartes’ maxim, “I think, therefore I am”, there is a social construction of self at work.<br><br>“You think of me, therefore I am.”<br><br>“I am your experience of me.”<br><br>“You are the stars in my cosmos. You are always there, watching me, comforting me. I don’t need a god to give me meaning. I have you.”<br><br>Bolano is working against the post-modern tendency toward solipsism and narcissism.<br><br>“You keep me alive by thinking and writing about me.”<br><br>Yet, perhaps the converse is also true: “I keep you alive by thinking and writing about you.”<br><br><b>Revolution and Freedom</b><br><br>Paradoxically, Bolano wrote the whole of the literary vehicle that makes this possible.<br><br>What are we to make of this?<br><br>Ultimately, literature is a social act, a form of social action.<br><br>What characterized the Visceral Poets was not just the fact that they wrote poetry, but that they were socially and politically engaged.<br><br>Their “generation all overdosed on Marx and Rimbaud”.<br><br>They rebelled against conformity, conservatism and the rigidity of tradition, both literary and political.<br><br>They wrote poetry that preserved youth, just as it preserved the present from the ravages of history.<br><br>They sought out the marvelous, when all around them was drab.<br><br>They resisted death as they resisted the passage of time.<br><br><b>Fiercely Modern</b><br><br>They rejected their own fathers and role models, becoming orphans on the way, not always discovering new fathers, except perhaps in fellow rebels such as Marx, Rimbaud, Trotsky, Gramsci, Lukacs, Althusser.<br><br>They found themselves trapped in diffuse labyrinths, so they travelled the world, navigating a sea of possibilities, exploring their spirit of adventure, seeking and finding youth, sex, love, friendship, experience, illumination and eventually death.<br><br>Then they realised that they were not alone, that Visceral Realism was not a destination, but just a mask that they wore on their journey toward modernity, that there had been others who sought modernity before them, the writer Borges, and the 20’s poet and mother of the Visceral Realists, Cesarea Tinajero, whose collected works they seek out in the passages that bookend the novel.<br><br><b>A Timeless Threnody</b><br><br>Bolano’s characters, like his own works, maintain a vigil over the body of life, literature and culture that lies dormant in the forgotten province of poets, essayists and professors.<br><br>They are a threnody that mourns our predecessors and their achievements, but somehow keeps them alive. <br><br>They create a dirge in what would otherwise be a void, a music that challenges silence, a being that defies nothingness.<br><br>Just as his characters look to the past, they inspire the future.<br><br>They become parents, mothers and fathers who inspire children and followers like the 17 year old Juan Garcia Madero.<br><br>They keep value alive and perpetuate it through the ages.<br><br>This is what Bolano did for us.<br><br>All he asks in return, now that he is dead, is that we do the same for those who follow us.<br><br><br><br><b>Quotation:</b><br><br><i>"The only real voyage consists not in seeking new landscapes, but in having new eyes; in seeing the universe through the eyes of another, one hundred others--in seeing the hundred universes that each of them sees."</i><br><br>Marcel Proust <br><br><br><br><b>Autor! Autor!</b><br><br>How did you do it?<br>You made me giggle and cry.<br>Holy Bolaño.<br><br><br><br><b>SOUNDTRACK:</b><br><br><b>Robyn Hitchcock and the Venus 3 – “Belltown Ramble”</b><br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=77ukY7CuAdg">http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=77ukY7...</a><br><br><br><i>"It's an independent life<br>And you wanna see your eyes<br>Reflected in the world.<br><br>"You can walk a square<br>You can walk an oblong<br>Even just walk straight.<br><br>"You'll still be standing there<br>Though you think you did the job wrong<br>You did it great."</i><br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=77ukY7CuAdg">http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=77ukY7...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="7796742403861555862" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/95699894" />
    <a title="Fabian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian"><img alt="Fabian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514061293p2/1036893.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/95699894?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian">Fabian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307476111" href="/book/show/7092772-los-detectives-salvajes">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer95699894" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10215149813680962767">Because it seems almost impossible to organize ideas for this review, in ode to the author I will present it as so:<br/><br/>March 1- : See "Savage Detectives" & "2666" (in Spanish) at Walmart (Yarbrough, El Paso, TX). Yes- it is easy to see the difference between the English books (Steele, Vampbooks by Meyer and Meyer-wannabes [hey, writers gotta eat too, you know:], magazines...) and the Spanish books (Gabriel G. Marquez, the Biblia). Because Liana told me to do so, I pick "Detectives salvajes."<br/><br/>March 1-</span>
  <span id="freeText10215149813680962767" style="display:none">Because it seems almost impossible to organize ideas for this review, in ode to the author I will present it as so:<br /><br />March 1- : See "Savage Detectives" &amp; "2666" (in Spanish) at Walmart (Yarbrough, El Paso, TX). Yes- it is easy to see the difference between the English books (Steele, Vampbooks by Meyer and Meyer-wannabes [hey, writers gotta eat too, you know:], magazines...) and the Spanish books (Gabriel G. Marquez, the Biblia). Because Liana told me to do so, I pick "Detectives salvajes."<br /><br />March 1- : At about page 150 (out of 609), I realize that before even though everything read like a very fresh, super-sublime Bret Easton Ellis creature of teenage doomdom, the novel soon cracks open and the range of possibilities seems infinite. The writer has you where he wants you.<br /><br />March 26: Reading accounts of more than 40 characters should bring on a headache but it doesn't. I could get one, though, at any minute. Too many names, addresses, congruent story lines (or even worse: non-connectivity of vignettes) COULD make me want to quit, but this novel is read like all the others: you must let go.<br /><br />March 30: There have been plenty of gems &amp; plenty of ?s.<br /><br />April 4: Almost done and it seems the conclusion will be--well, not what expected.<br /><br />May 1: Bought "2666"<br /><br />So, this one smells like a classic. It (almost) reaches the heights no one else even, well, imagined. The author makes his own expectations and smiles as he shatters them for his open-mouthed readers all along the way. There is an entire world constructed by Mr. Bolano and though not all characters inspire sympathy, nor are all that realistic, nor may have any purpose in the narrative whatsoever, many are like my (and probably your) friends. Here is the theme: that art is alive, that those that suffer for it, those that live for it, can only they themselves construct meaning out of life. There is an odyssey (do NOT tell me Ulises Lima ain't a quixotean Ulysses, or Homer's Odysseus) which is bravely taken by those that realize this beauty, this radical movement of the Americas to make poetry and establish a much needed Renaissance; they travel everywhere like nomads and never settle. They read, exchange ideas, &amp; produce.<br /><br />Certainly, what Bolano is trying to tell us here is that there is some literature out there that yearns to be discovered.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10215149813680962767" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_17527717" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/17527717" />
    <a title="Rod" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/304548-rod"><img alt="Rod" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515377662p2/304548.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/17527717?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rod" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rod" href="/user/show/304548-rod">Rod</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312427481" href="/book/show/1940878.The_Savage_Detectives">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer17527717" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2176976002638591706">I have a good feeling about this, based on the first few pages. Feels like Murakami meets Kerouac. So, grown up Roddy reading meets teenage Roddy reading. The locale shifts from Japan and the USA to South and Central America. The quest narrative continues with a new backdrop. Everybody wins. <br/><br/>************<br/><br/>300 pages later, and nothing has happened yet, so I'm having second thoughts about my first impression. It's not at all clear what the big deal is supposed to be about this book. I mean, seriousl</span>
  <span id="freeText2176976002638591706" style="display:none">I have a good feeling about this, based on the first few pages. Feels like Murakami meets Kerouac. So, grown up Roddy reading meets teenage Roddy reading. The locale shifts from Japan and the USA to South and Central America. The quest narrative continues with a new backdrop. Everybody wins. <br /><br />************<br /><br />300 pages later, and nothing has happened yet, so I'm having second thoughts about my first impression. It's not at all clear what the big deal is supposed to be about this book. I mean, seriously, nothing has happened. And it isn't even as if nothing has happened particularly artfully or lyrically. It's a very prosaic kind of nothing. I'm going to keep reading, because I hate to give up, but this is not good. I still have 300 pages to go, and there just seems to be a lot more nothing coming at me. and how exactly is any of this "postmodern" or "experimental," as some of the reviewers have suggested? This seems to be one of the very least experimental books you could imagine, unless typing is considered somehow innovative (cf. Jonathan Safran Foer, another really good typist, but not so much of a good novelist). In many respects this novel is like a Stephen Malkmus album. All the hipsters claim to like it, so you check it out, only to find that it's a really really long series of in-jokes that isn't funny or interesting in the least. In some cultures this is called wanking.<br /><br />**************<br /><br />Finally, the long nightmare is over. I can fairly safely say that this is one of the very worst books I've ever read, which is all the more disappointing considering how excited I was about reading it in the first place. Nothing continued to happen for another 300 pages, until we ended up literally staring at an empty box. The next time I want to read about nothing I'll get out the Beckett. Nothing happens a lot more elegantly and succinctly there. I haven't been this mad about a reading experience since Bonfire of the Vanities.<br /><br />Can I give a book no stars?</span>
  <a data-text-id="2176976002638591706" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_66560429" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/66560429" />
    <a title="Geoff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/126266-geoff"><img alt="Geoff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1399505224p2/126266.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/66560429?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Geoff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Geoff" href="/user/show/126266-geoff">Geoff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer66560429" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9974972816323488808">~~<br/><br/>To do anything well we must do it until death, and we won't have gotten closer to perfection for all of that; and it is sad that most of us don't do anything more than what was asked of us by something else a long time ago; and so our energies are dispersed and lost and we are less for our efforts. Not to serve another and not to serve the Self but to serve the inner Void where all infinities collide and collapse. If we followed what calls to us most desperately we'd all be wanderers, or we'd </span>
  <span id="freeText9974972816323488808" style="display:none">~~<br /><br />To do anything well we must do it until death, and we won't have gotten closer to perfection for all of that; and it is sad that most of us don't do anything more than what was asked of us by something else a long time ago; and so our energies are dispersed and lost and we are less for our efforts. Not to serve another and not to serve the Self but to serve the inner Void where all infinities collide and collapse. If we followed what calls to us most desperately we'd all be wanderers, or we'd never leave the arms of the people we love, or we'd carry those people with us as we go. The world intervenes on our plans and silly dreams and makes disasters of all of our lives; the most stable of us are made stable by the will of another, and our stability is the residue of compromise. Mortality is only reckoned with by a few, who seem to us criminals, geniuses, vagabonds, soldiers in the cause of some ghostly ideal, spoken on the tail end of a wind radiating from oblivion. Strain to hear it, and we wander off and vanish. We hope to live honestly, but we can't even define our desires in this field of noise through which we wade. So maybe History, or Tragedy, which are one and the same, intervene as the first cause, initiating what is unable to be initiated when we are just sitting here alone with ourselves motioning silently through thick, gelatinous waves of existence. I see the works of Bolaño as the beautiful rubble of a man who fought to serve the inner Void and who stared down the wind as to fix it to his eyes, hold it, a task at which of course he failed; all of us who try to hold the truth fail; prismatic shards are our recompense. We gather the shards and ingest them and they slice our innards to pieces. But this is growth. When we disappear, something else becomes real. There is comfort in that.<br /><br />~</span>
  <a data-text-id="9974972816323488808" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_276096165" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/276096165" />
    <a title="Darwin8u" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u"><img alt="Darwin8u" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547440402p2/2294090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/276096165?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darwin8u" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darwin8u" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u">Darwin8u</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=2014">2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer276096165" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11684881591957746627"><i>“Nothing happened today. And if anything did, I’d rather not talk about it, because I didn’t understand it.”</i> <br/> ― Roberto Bolaño, The Savage Detectives<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1415981468i/11871725._SY540_.png' width='400' height='400' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><i>“In some lost fold of the past, we wanted to be lions and we're no more than castrated cats”</i> <br/> ― Roberto Bolaño, The Savage Detectives<br/><br/>This is a book that is nearly impossible to review, absolutely impossible to summarize, and simultaneously amazing and frustrating. Bolaño created a novel and a narrative that (IMHO) attempted to capture the energy, th</span>
  <span id="freeText11684881591957746627" style="display:none"><i>“Nothing happened today. And if anything did, I’d rather not talk about it, because I didn’t understand it.”</i> <br> ― Roberto Bolaño, The Savage Detectives<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1415981468i/11871725._SY540_.png" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>“In some lost fold of the past, we wanted to be lions and we're no more than castrated cats”</i> <br> ― Roberto Bolaño, The Savage Detectives<br><br>This is a book that is nearly impossible to review, absolutely impossible to summarize, and simultaneously amazing and frustrating. Bolaño created a novel and a narrative that (IMHO) attempted to capture the energy, the personalities, the youth and the mortar that held together Mexican and Latin American poets during the mid-1970s. It feels like he took every poetic image, idea, stray hair and paper from every Mexican poet during the past forty years and laid them all down on black velvet to be examined. He found poetry in the "visceral realists" excesses and his semi-autobiographical confessions. Bolaño jumps from chapter-to-chapter, from scene-to-scene, from sunset-to-sunset and keeps reinventing his PoMo novel as he writes it. <br><br>I have to be fair. It wasn't my favorite novel, but it seems the most likely (of all the novels I've read these last two or three years) to suddenly become animated. If any novel is going to jump off my lap, and wander off into the wilderness -- this is the one. It seems to be written not just in ink, but in blood, tears, seed, and fire.<br><br>It someways it reminds me of the beginning of Yeat's poem 'Second Coming': <br><br><i>Turning and turning in the widening gyre<br>The falcon cannot hear the falconer;<br>Things fall apart; the centre cannot hold;<br>Mere anarchy is loosed upon the world,<br>The blood-dimmed tide is loosed, and everywhere<br>The ceremony of innocence is drowned;<br>The best lack all conviction, while the worst<br>Are full of passionate intensity.</i><br><br>With Roberto Bolaño the center of this gyre is Mexico City and with each page he writes (forward and back in time) Bolaño seems to be adding potential energy to the explosion that will loose his mad, Mexican poets; these thieves and dealers, these visceral realists, around the world. As I chew on this image, I think the idea of vortexes and gyres is equally applicable to ALL poets. It captures the way creativity often explodes, demands to be exposed, and drives before its flood chariots of innocence, creativity and youth. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11684881591957746627" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_118260478" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/118260478" />
    <a title="Mike Puma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4193758-mike-puma"><img alt="Mike Puma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1434667514p2/4193758.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/118260478?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike Puma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike Puma" href="/user/show/4193758-mike-puma">Mike Puma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312427481" href="/book/show/1940878.The_Savage_Detectives">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">James Wood</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4193758-mike-puma?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4193758-mike-puma?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4193758-mike-puma?shelf=lit-fic">lit-fic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4193758-mike-puma?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4193758-mike-puma?shelf=2009">2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4193758-mike-puma?shelf=bola%C3%B1o">bolaño</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4193758-mike-puma?shelf=chilean-author">chilean-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer118260478" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1994378176628557368"><p>This review, such as it is, might be considered <i>spoilerish</i>, actually, it’s a lotta spoilerish, it’s presented in a rambling, perhaps, incoherent manner, and it is tentatively offered. It also includes a speculative consideration, for your reading enjoyment—one you’re very entitled to disagree with. Take a little theory, take a little text, stir them together, you get speculation. Toward that end I focus on a single aspect of the novel. You’ve been warned.</p><p>Ladies and Gentlemen, you want to know wh</p></span>
  <span id="freeText1994378176628557368" style="display:none"><p>This review, such as it is, might be considered <i>spoilerish</i>, actually, it’s a lotta spoilerish, it’s presented in a rambling, perhaps, incoherent manner, and it is tentatively offered. It also includes a speculative consideration, for your reading enjoyment—one you’re very entitled to disagree with. Take a little theory, take a little text, stir them together, you get speculation. Toward that end I focus on a single aspect of the novel. You’ve been warned.</p><p>Ladies and Gentlemen, you want to know what Visceral Realism is? Listen to this, one of the great songs (one of my ‘desert island picks’-- any version). <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=B2rjDBQ1UDY">http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=B2rjDB...</a> The guttural (bass) at play with the high-soaring (the stellar Mitchell vocal &amp; lyric). Play it again, follow Jaco Pastorius’ bass line. Play it again. You can feel it; it will own you. JP, gone too soon. RB, gone too soon. Life sux, but they’ve left us their music.</p><p>Okay. I wondered how to begin this thing, there it is. It works or it doesn’t, but you can’t fault the song—not without being a dick.</p> <p>This is the second time I’ve read TSD, and this time I read it <i>differently</i>, for lack of a better term, I read it more slowly, closely if you will, things began to appear to me that hadn’t with the first reading. I started compiling a list of all the proper names, with indications when I knew the names were real, but several chapters into the second section I knew I wouldn’t be able to maintain the list and finish reading the novel any time soon. I started keeping running summaries of the entries in section two (The Savage Detectives), and something interesting became apparent, actually unapparent, that created dissonance—which led me to a single conclusion: this is a book about Legend Making. Creating a legend. How fact (fictive fact) and myth (fictive myth) and creative license combine to create Legend. </p><p>Everyone knows Arturo Belano is Bolaño’s alter-ego—his fictive self. With The Savage Detectives, Bolaño creates his mythic self, his self as he wishes to be seen, his self as he knows others have seen him, perhaps even the self he hoped to never be. The life of Bolaño and Belano so closely intertwined, most of us will never know where one varies from the other; a double-helix, the germ cell of a Legend.</p><p>For the handful who don’t know what this novel is, I’ll provide the briefest summary I can: Parts I &amp; III are Juan García Madero’s diary entries chronicling how he, Arturo Belano, Ulises Lima and Lupe, the prostitute, met in Mexico City and fled to the Sonoran Desert— to escape Lupe’s pimp (Lupe and García Madero) and to find a lost literary hero of the Visceral Realist movement, Cesárea Tinajero (Belano and Lima); Part II is a series of oral histories/testamonies/interviews (less the questions and the voice of an interviewer) presented in the chronological order in which they were made except for one which seems to have occurred in a single telling but split into sections across chapters—the order of the entries has little or nothing to do with the narrative chronology. My interest is Part II—the troublesome Part II.</p><p>Why troublesome? To begin with, the story of Amadeo Salvatierra (dated January 1976): in an extended ‘testimony’ which spans 13 of the section’s 26 chapters, Salvatierra recounts the night and morning spent with Ulises Lima and Arturo Belano, drinking heavily, discussing Cesárea Tinajero, and analyzing the only poem of her’s Salvatierra has; Lima and Belano explain to Salvatierra that the poem is a joke. Why is this troublesome then? Salvatierra tells his story while Lima and Belano are off in the desert, far, far away from Mexico City. Hold that thought.</p><p>Unlike the Salvatierra testimony and others from January ‘76, the entry from Andrés Ramirez (Barcelona, Dec. ’88) is clearly addressed to Belano; while the interviewer’s questions are omitted, the responses are to Belano (“I was destined to be a failure, Belano, take my word for it.” “I know you’ve been in similar situations, Belano, so I won’t go on too long.”) Nor will I, but hold that thought. In the penultimate interview, and it’s clearly an interview addressed to an anonymous ‘sir,’ Ernesto García Grajales (Dec. ’96) summarizes what became of the Visceral Realists premised on the research he’s done for a book: “Yes, you could say I’m the foremost scholar in the field, [visceral realism/visceral realists] the definitive authority, but that’s not saying much. I’m probably the only person who cares.” Yet, he’s unaware of Juan García Madero, introduces a poet called Bustamente, and doesn’t know much about Belano. If the interviewer was, in fact, Belano, I suspect he’d regard this interview as something of a joke, like the poem of Cesárea Tinajero. Hold that thought.</p><p>Any and every great Detective story includes one thing—a mystery. Hold that thought.</p><p> Now with all those thoughts in mind, the question becomes: Who are the Savage Detectives? Are they Belano and Lima on their search for the illusive Cesárea Tinajero? On their searches for something else? Are they the unnamed interviewers of the various testimonies in section II (which would include Belano)? Or, might they be someone else? </p><p>From <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/89419.Wayne_C__Booth" title="Wayne C. Booth" rel="nofollow noopener">Wayne C. Booth</a> we learn (perhaps, more than we’d like) about the Implied Author. From <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/206971.Wolfgang_Iser" title="Wolfgang Iser" rel="nofollow noopener">Wolfgang Iser</a> we learn (perhaps, more than we’d like) about the Implied Reader. All well and good. Now comes the Implied Editor from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/4193758-mike-puma" rel="nofollow noopener">this guy</a>. Unless we assume that Bolaño was sloppy with his timeline, we have to believe someone asked Salvatierra to account for his night with Lima and Belano—someone other than Belano, as during January, 1976, Belano was chasing all over the desert and there’s no indication he’d contacted anyone from the road. So the testimonial was solicited by someone else—but who would have cared in January, 1976? It could have been Alberto the pimp or his policeman accomplice, but neither would have pursued further testimonies after early February. At least some of the testimonies were addressed to Belano, although it’s unlikely he would have crossed paths with narrators who only knew Lima in remote locations. Who initiated these leftover, unaccounted for testimonies? Who is the Implied Editor/detective who edited out the questions posed to these characters? Who could this other ‘detective’ be? And what makes him/her ‘savage’? Who is this Implied Editor? The one who’s taken all the various pieces, strands, stories of known origin but unknown behest, and determinedly (savagely?) attempts to make sense of them? Create the Bolaño/Belano Legend? Isn’t it just possible that we have seen the Savage Detectives and they are us?</p><p>One of the titles from my Favorites shelf, do I really need to tell you <i>how much</i> I like it? There are scads of great reviews for TSD, covering themes, impressions, and how Bolaño fits into the mindscapes of the various reviewers. Read them all; they’re worth it.</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="1994378176628557368" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1464804821" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1464804821" />
    <a title="Edward" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12933866-edward"><img alt="Edward" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444136022p2/12933866.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1464804821?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward" href="/user/show/12933866-edward">Edward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330445153" href="/book/show/3303801-the-savage-detectives">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=2019">2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1464804821" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1506631838040481053">What differentiates Bolaño from other much-loved authors is that he does not have a singular, distinctive style by which he can be universally recognised. I have found in my experience, and from reading the reviews of others, that having enjoyed one Bolaño novel is no guarantee that you will enjoy the next. In fact, given the range of styles and approaches he employs, perhaps a correspondingly wide range of responses is also to be expected. So in a way when we talk about a shared appreciation of</span>
  <span id="freeText1506631838040481053" style="display:none">What differentiates Bolaño from other much-loved authors is that he does not have a singular, distinctive style by which he can be universally recognised. I have found in my experience, and from reading the reviews of others, that having enjoyed one Bolaño novel is no guarantee that you will enjoy the next. In fact, given the range of styles and approaches he employs, perhaps a correspondingly wide range of responses is also to be expected. So in a way when we talk about a shared appreciation of Bolaño’s writing, it may be the case that we are all talking about different things.<br /> <br />If there is such a thing as a Rosetta Stone for Bolaño, it is this novel: <i>The Savage Detectives</i> seems to compress and showcase many of the diverse features of his writing. The first and last sections of the novel employ the direct, journalistic structure of <i>The Third Reich</i>, while the lengthy central section utilises the multi-viewpoint oral history structure of <i>The Skating Rink</i>, which reveals the story gradually, almost incidentally, while building a larger universe (perhaps more accurately: a multitude of universes that bump and coalesce to form a volatile broth). The novel contains in one of its chapters the entire summarised plot of <i>Amulet</i>, and its major themes concerning literature as a fundamental component of life recalls <i>Last Evenings on Earth</i>. The entire central section of the novel is a relentless variation on a theme – also one of Bolano’s hallmarks - which is reminiscent of <i>Nazi Literature in the Americas</i> and the much-despised fourth part of <i>2666</i>. These chapters are rich, though difficult, and here one must be prepared to become the Detective of the novel, uncover the story piece by piece, while accepting that it is impossible to reveal all the connections. <br /> <br />All of these things make <i>The Savage Detectives</i> a fairly challenging read, especially if you are someone unfamiliar with Bolaño’. If you have read this novel and felt mixed reactions, this is perfectly reasonable, and I hope the preceding paragraph can help indicate which of his other novels you may enjoy more, and which to avoid. For me, I appreciated the synthesis of <i>The Savage Detectives</i>, which encompassed my favourite aspects of Bolaño, while transcending those I enjoy less. This is the eighth Bolaño novel I’ve read and perhaps the last I will read for some time. It felt like a culmination, an apotheosis, and (strangely, given it is actually one of his earlier novels) something of a resolution.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1506631838040481053" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_400476591" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/400476591" />
    <a title="Garima" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7915003-garima"><img alt="Garima" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1360053697p2/7915003.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/400476591?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Garima" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Garima" href="/user/show/7915003-garima">Garima</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=its-not-you-its-meta-or-gfhrytyt">its-not-you-its-meta-or-gfhrytyt</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer400476591" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17312653420445010324"><i>The innocence of childhood, the muddiness of adolescence, the charm of youth<br/><br/>Unconditional love of a mother, passionate love of a lover, bloody revenge by an enemy.<br/><br/>Teachings of a teacher, lessons learnt by a student, choosing a road untraveled.<br/><br/>Poems by poets, novels by writers, paintings by painters.<br/><br/>A lost idol, reminiscences by ironic souls, A regained Idol.<br/><br/>Love, obsession, sex, drugs, heart-breaks, longing, road-trip, search, survival.<br/><br/>Arturo Belano, Roberto Bolano, Ulises Lima, Mario Santiago- </i></span>
  <span id="freeText17312653420445010324" style="display:none"><i>The innocence of childhood, the muddiness of adolescence, the charm of youth<br><br>Unconditional love of a mother, passionate love of a lover, bloody revenge by an enemy.<br><br>Teachings of a teacher, lessons learnt by a student, choosing a road untraveled.<br><br>Poems by poets, novels by writers, paintings by painters.<br><br>A lost idol, reminiscences by ironic souls, A regained Idol.<br><br>Love, obsession, sex, drugs, heart-breaks, longing, road-trip, search, survival.<br><br>Arturo Belano, Roberto Bolano, Ulises Lima, Mario Santiago- <b>The Savage Detectives</b></i><br><br><b>WARNING</b>: Reading The Savage Detectives can be injurious to Readers’ health. It contains symptoms of awesomeness, which are difficult to contain in words invented by English language, thus have the evil power to make you suffer from the dreaded <b>REVIEWER’S BLOCK</b> disease. With proper rest, retail therapy, finding solace in other books (less awesome ones) might help you overcome your pitiful condition. Since I’m feeling a bit better now, I am attempting this review. Kindly co-operate if I come across as somewhat deranged or childlike excited.<br><br><b>
  <u>REVIEW</u>
</b><br><br>A while back, God knows what I was looking for on net and what magical words I typed in Google search bar in my sleep that I stumbled upon this page about neglected books:<br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://neglectedbooks.com/">http://neglectedbooks.com/</a><br><br>I didn’t browse through it at that time but remember myself feeling sad just from the name itself. Thinking about all those writers whose books are featured on the site made me wonder about the artists who dedicate themselves to various arts like writing, poetry, painting, music, acting, film-making, et al and how many of them actually achieve in their respective fields to be known in the world and how many simply get lost in oblivion and move on to something clichéd to earn a living, just because they tried to live their dreams and got no one to acknowledge them.<br><br>Citing an trivial example, even here on Goodreads, I have come across some brilliant reviews lying somewhere on 10th page or 67th, with 0 likes or comments rendered unheeded due to lack of some other skills may be. This was all Before reading this book and then I read IT. <br><br>With the turn of 1st page of 1st part, I braced myself for a fascinating journey that was about to begin. And as expected, this book turned out to be a literary Time machine tugging me back and forth among various temporal shifts belonging to different species of human beings starting with the 1st part, MEXICANS LOST IN MEXICO which was decorative enough with the diary entries of a young fucked (literally) up budding poet, Juan Garcia Madero. A big time messed up or simply confused, he found his way through the supposedly fun, revolutionary, oxymoronic named poetry group, Visceral Realists. A chance encounter at a poetry workshop introduced him to king-pins of the present day visceral realism, Arturo Belano (Not Bolano) and Ulises (not Ulysses) Lima, an introduction that further led to getting acquainted with an assortment of normal, abnormal, homosexuals, prostitutes, pimps, pornographers, junkies, cuckolds, sleep talkers, and other virtuous kinds of dudes and dudettes, all of them are prime examples of what one should NOT be like in their youth.<br><br>In the process, he lost his virginity in epic style, developed the ability to be unfaithful and eventually entrapped in an event where the phrase, in the wrong place at the wrong time seems apt for him. And abruptly his diary ends at a cliff hanger to make way for 2nd part, THE SAVAGE DETECTIVES, and savage they were but in an endearing manner (whateva that’s supposed to mean). This part is purportedly a profound confession by a poet (Roberto Bolano) who ironically became famous as a writer and a confession made on behalf of his friend expressed through various voices having nothing great to say about those poets (except few) as they were busy recollecting the peripheral circumstances that brought the presence of either of them in their lives and saying, “and then I met him”. A simple fact that they could recall them was proof enough of their existence. <br><br>And here comes the most feared question.<br><br><b>Why do you like The Savage Detectives? Explain. <br><br>Ans. </b>When I decided to read this book, I had some pre-conceived notions about it, that it’s going to be a tough read, difficult to comprehend and painfully long to get finished and by applying Speed, distance and time formulae (not really!) I allotted it a month’s time to complete. But Lo and Behold, I found myself reading it at every possible opportunity, while waiting for a friend, between job breaks, amidst refreshing the Goodreads Homepage and of course by stealing few hours of sleep. It was like I was meant to read this book, as if it was written for Me especially and when you feel like that you know you are in for a life-long friendship. <br><br>Every testimonial/ interview/ reminiscence in the book conveys a subtle facet of our past, present and future. You can’t help to feel a deep connection with one thing or the other in more than one way. The impalpable aspects of a situation or the whole story, leaves a deep mark on your being, making you ask yourself questions, analyzing the answers you somehow manage to give and present to you your life report card with comments at the bottom with heading : Possible areas of improvement or sorry, better luck next time, If there will be any.<br><br>When I mentioned, <i>the prime examples of what one should not be like in their youth</i>, I was just being sardonic. So what is youth exactly?<br><br>Some Definitions :<br><br>youth (<i>yooth</i>)<br><br>- the quality or condition of being young, immature, or inexperienced<br><br>- the period between childhood and maturity, esp adolescence and early adulthood<br><br>- the freshness, vigour, or vitality characteristic of young people<br><br>But one definition that is most suitable is that Youth is state of <b>temporary permanency</b>. It defines a huge part of our existence and it defined this book for its readers. Among all the revolutionary talk about the world of literature and Poetry, it slowly and steadily captures the essence of that endless ocean whereupon the ship of our life is sailing, tackling the rough waves or going smoothly over calm sea. <br><br>In the words of Mojo risin, <i>“All the poetry has wolves in it”</i><br><br>And in the words of Roberto Bolano,<i>” Literature is no innocent.”</i><br><br>This book is no innocent neither the writing of Bolano. His relentless prose won’t pamper you in any way. The writing is perfectly imperfect and complicated in the most sublime fashion. The backdrop though is the poetry world or arts in general; in actual it’s through various people associated directly or indirectly with that art, those who reeve the rope of life through pearls of emotions and feelings at different stages of life governed by love, madness, narcissism, loneliness or senility, that the main essence of this book delineates for me because anything apart from obvious joy or happy endings is where spirit of living rests for many. <br><br>The main characters are like shadows in most of the narratives defined for me through this quote:<br><br><i>“But I didn’t see his face, just his shadow as it crossed the bar. A shadow empty of metaphor, evoking nothing, a shadow that was only a shadow with no wish to be something else.”</i><br><br>I must admit that there are parts that bored me to some extent but BUT:<br><br><i>” Do you know what the worst thing about literature is?....That you end up being friends with writers. And friendship, treasure though it may be, destroys your critical sense.”</i><br><br>Ain’t Bolano one helluva smart fella!<br><br>This book is your story, my story and everyone’s story and isn’t it nice when you get to READ one. <br><br>What’s inside this book?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380389032i/749979._SY540_.jpg" width="300" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Ans.</b> LIFE as we knew it! <br></span>
  <a data-text-id="17312653420445010324" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_101686658" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101686658" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/101686658?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330509527" href="/book/show/6586229-the-savage-detectives">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2006-2010)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-core">1001-core</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=doorstopper">doorstopper</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=latin-american">latin-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101686658" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15585864059559385738">I enjoyed <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3115359.2666' title='2666 by Roberto Bolaño' rel='nofollow noopener'>2666</a>(5 stars) more than this. <i>2666</i> is more engaging, brutal and with far more interesting characters. <i>2666</i> is also more cohesive and the plot is more intricately built. However, <i>The Savage Detectives</i> has more heart being basically a story of a male friendship. Then what made this friendship between two poets <b>Arturo Belano</b> and <b>Ulises Lima</b> more touching was the fact that this was based on real-life friendship between Roberto Bolano (as Arturo Belano) and his friend Mario Santiago (as Uli</span>
  <span id="freeText15585864059559385738" style="display:none">I enjoyed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3115359.2666" title="2666 by Roberto Bolaño" rel="nofollow noopener">2666</a>(5 stars) more than this. <i>2666</i> is more engaging, brutal and with far more interesting characters. <i>2666</i> is also more cohesive and the plot is more intricately built. However, <i>The Savage Detectives</i> has more heart being basically a story of a male friendship. Then what made this friendship between two poets <b>Arturo Belano</b> and <b>Ulises Lima</b> more touching was the fact that this was based on real-life friendship between Roberto Bolano (as Arturo Belano) and his friend Mario Santiago (as Ulises Lima).<br /><br />This book is very popular among my foreigner friends here in Goodreads. So I will not write a long detailed synopsis of the plot anymore. If <i>2666</i> has 5 sub-parts, this has only 3. The first and the last are narrated by a young poet called <b>Juan Garcia Madero</b> while the middle part is narrated by 50+ people who have at some points in their lives crossed the paths of the Belano and Lima. Think of a long queue during a eulogy for a popular man. It's so happened that the people there deliver no-holds-barred eulogies. No-holds-barred because the people who speak in front of the coffin say not just the good things the dead man did did while he was still alive but even the bad ones, the dead man's intimate secrets and even their unfounded opinions.<br /><br />Roberto Bolano was a genius. He was part of the new batch of Latin American writers who gave a fresher feel on the novels in that part of the world that used to be dominated by the magical realism writers led by my favorite Gabriel Garcia Marquez. Their styles are different and I do not want to take sides. I love them both. <br /><br />Bolano was also one of those writers who are not afraid to write long novels. He said that "the longer the writer's book is, the higher is the chance that inconsistencies can come out." However, he was not afraid to expose himself, precisely because there was no inconsistency in both long novels of him that I've read. His storytelling is flawless and his imagination is boundless. There is basically no fault that I could cite in either of these two wonderful works.<br /><br />My next read will definitely be his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/63029.Amulet" title="Amulet by Roberto Bolaño" rel="nofollow noopener">Amulet</a>. Then because of this <i>The Savage Detectives</i>, I will buy his other book: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/63031.By_Night_in_Chile" title="By Night in Chile by Roberto Bolaño" rel="nofollow noopener">By Night in Chile</a>. When will this buying and reading ever stop?</span>
  <a data-text-id="15585864059559385738" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_412975217" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/412975217" />
    <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls"><img alt="MJ Nicholls" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374341850p2/2386804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/412975217?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MJ Nicholls" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls">MJ Nicholls</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330445153" href="/book/show/3303801-the-savage-detectives">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=voluminous">voluminous</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=tortured-artists">tortured-artists</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=latin-american">latin-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer412975217" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4420101826787610479">I am told this novel made some minor splash upon its publication. I see no evidence to support this claim. I see no particular swelling of interest in this lowly text on Goodreads. I see no ecstatic over-the-top declarations of lust for this novel. No effusive dissertations conveying the message “I totally bought into the hype and splooged fifty times over this book like Ron Jeremy catching his reflection in the pupils of a malnourished Cuban trollop.” I see no substantial body of scholarship ag</span>
  <span id="freeText4420101826787610479" style="display:none">I am told this novel made some minor splash upon its publication. I see no evidence to support this claim. I see no particular swelling of interest in this lowly text on Goodreads. I see no ecstatic over-the-top declarations of lust for this novel. No effusive dissertations conveying the message “I totally bought into the hype and splooged fifty times over this book like Ron Jeremy catching his reflection in the pupils of a malnourished Cuban trollop.” I see no substantial body of scholarship agglutinating on the first two review pages alone. I see no pitiful deniers, squeaking their dissenting humbuggery about the overrated and overhyped nature of the prose and so on and boo-hoo, swallowed up in box after box of Bolaño devotees on their knees licking the long-dead man’s Chilean loafers as though hoping to absorb some essence of the punchdrunk poet’s furious pace, first-person range and painful aversion to paragraph breaks. I see no evidence of this whatso— Oh no, wait . . . there they are. Oops.<br /><br />What of this? A structural sandwich. The bread: a road-trip narrative about a poetry nerd with a penchant for obscure technical words for verse forms and metrical structures that explodes into violence. The filling: an <i>n</i>th number of first-person interview-style intersecting stories about the short-lived Mexican experimental poetry movement visceral realism. More unreliable narrators than the Bible. More icky sex than a caterpillar’s boudoir. More characters per page than <i>Catch-22</i>. A Mexican <i>Thousand and One Nights</i> of tales, yarns, confessions, digressions, hoodwinks, self-reference, neverending stories and long blog-like rambles. A personally insulting deficit of paragraph breaks. An entertained but infuriated MJ. A far-too-long second part which this gringo abandoned on p481 to move into the final section (which he left on p550 due to mounting boredom). A loquacious universe-sized novel of sprawling scope and ambition that collapses under its own weight but leaves an indelible imprint on the reader’s psyche. An aperitif compared to the five-square-meals of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/63032.2666" title="2666 by Roberto Bolaño" rel="nofollow noopener">2666</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4420101826787610479" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_182632926" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/182632926" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/182632926?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand">1-read-on-hand</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-everything">person-of-everything</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-read">antidote-think-twice-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-all">antidote-think-twice-all</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=spanish">spanish</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-translated">person-of-translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=translated">translated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer182632926" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3473313895183842510">I hate the description for this novel. Anything longer than a single paragraph is destined for bloviation, an Excel graph of key phrases selling itself to as many bidders as possible. A long list of characters fishing for the lay reader's empathy? Borges and Pynchon for those who don't need that sort of nonsense? Please. If it gets more people reading Bolaño, sure, but these days that's the end all excuse for literature in a capitalist society. The least we can do is point it out and follow it b</span>
  <span id="freeText3473313895183842510" style="display:none">I hate the description for this novel. Anything longer than a single paragraph is destined for bloviation, an Excel graph of key phrases selling itself to as many bidders as possible. A long list of characters fishing for the lay reader's empathy? Borges and Pynchon for those who don't need that sort of nonsense? Please. If it gets more people reading Bolaño, sure, but these days that's the end all excuse for literature in a capitalist society. The least we can do is point it out and follow it back to its sordid origins, especially for a book such as this, one that follows the trail of wannabe written word devotees and doesn't tune out a single one.<blockquote>
  <i> “To a great extent,” he confessed, “everything that I’ve written is a love letter or a farewell letter to my own generation.”<br /><br />-“Between Parentheses”</i>
</blockquote>There. That's all I would need on the cover flap or Goodreads description. I knew barely anything about Bolaño when I started this, even less than when I read <i>2666</i> four or five years ago, because at least then I was a blank slate. Nowadays my desires for reading are flighty and irritatingly complex, and what I was seeing in this description that I so despise was not the Bolaño I felt behind that cover and in that Part Four but <i>On the Road</i>, some self-aggrandizing shit with bonus literature meanderings and gore porn for depth.<blockquote>
  <i>I'm not trying to justify myself. I'm just trying to tell a story.</i>
</blockquote>This book has three parts, and the first didn't help. Or rather, it did, but it makes me grateful that I finish everything I start, because the Murakami comparisons were becoming increasingly accurate in all the wrong ways and a solipsistic boy receptacle of poetry terminology and bad sex is not my idea of quality literature until, of course, the book grew up. Twenty years the description says, forgetting what happens to those college age dreams of reading and writing in twenty years, omitting any mention of a generation coming to terms with the fact that the love for an ideal doesn't pay. Copy editing does. Cashiering does. Teaching does, as does law, and business, and following the commercial fervor of the masses. Not art.<blockquote>
  <i>Literature isn't innocent.</i>
</blockquote>No one can live on a revolution, no matter how bloodless or inspirational. Let us speak of famous men, then let us mention their inherited income and every other birthright advantage that sailed their names down to us, the luck of a moment and society's requisites for a livelihood. Notice the lack of women, and any number of unnamed dead. The description mentions violence, but that is not the same as your hairs rising at the mention of Pinochet, Chile in 1973, or Santa Teresa, Ciudad Juárez for those of you who haven't yet read <i>2666</i>.<blockquote>
  <i>Life left us all where we were meant to be or where it was convenient to leave us and then forgot us, which is as it should be.</i>
</blockquote>Murakami comes back for his <i>Kafka on the Shore</i> questioning of violence, operating in tandem with Vollmann for his ubiquitous empathy without a trace of sentiment. That list of characters in the description tells you nothing of how Bolaño writes them, mythologizing himself as only human in order to give voice with respectful dignity to everyone else. I'll give partial credit to the description for mentioning Pynchon, but the coupling with Borges is unforgivable. I go to Borges when I want skeleton nerves in an ivory tomb belying the very marrow of my existence. Wannabe written word devotee that I am, on the cusp of transition with all the requisite baggage, that marrow is Bolaño.<blockquote>
  <i>Some unemployed person could reproach me for being incapable of happiness, even though I had everything. I could reproach a murderer for committing murders, and a murderer could reproach a suicide for his desperate or enigmatic last act. The truth is that one day it was all over and I took a look around me.<br /><br />All languages seemed detestable to me just then.</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="3473313895183842510" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
